Original Text:In Agent RL, models suffer from Template Collapse. They generate vast, diverse outputs (High Entropy) that lose all meaningful connection to the input prompt (Low Mutual Information). In other words, agent learn different ways to say nothing. 🚀 Introducing RAGEN-v2 -- Here's how we define and fix such silent failure modes in Agent RL. 🧵

@imhaotian · 2 actions

Original Text:I left xAI earlier this week. It was a difficult decision. The past two years have been an intense, fun, and deeply rewarding journey, and I accomplished things I could not have imagined two years ago. Thank you @elonmusk for the opportunity and for everything I learned at xAI. Thank you @Guodzh for the trust you placed in me and for all the days and late nights we worked through together. And thank you to the entire Omni / Imagine team: thank you for your trust, and for growing together with me. It has been an honor, and I am incredibly proud of what we achieved together. I feel fortunate to have had the chance to work with all of you. At xAI, everything feels possible. I had the chance to work with and learn from some of the most exceptional people I have ever met. I was able to explore across domains: from pretraining to post-training, from language models to multimodal, from perception to generation. Joining xAI was one of the best decisions I have ever made. @grok imagine is special to me. Building video generation models, where I started with almost zero prior knowledge, from 0 to No.1, as an IC and as a lead, alongside an extraordinary team, and shipping it as a great product used by millions, all within 6 months, at age 28: I feel proud. But now it’s time for me to move on. I’m burnt out, and I know my happiness is no longer maximized in my current state. It is sad to say goodbye, but it is just the right time for a change. Best wishes to the Imagine team, you are absolutely the best, and you deserve the best. I will cherish all our memories for the rest of my life. For now, I’m taking a break and giving myself time to figure out what comes next. Posted from Hawaii.

Original Post #2032334282937811040

@ilex_ulmus · 1 actions

▸Click to expand/collapse

Original Text:@jachiam0 @GWHayduke97 We already know a majority of people don’t like frontier AI and scientific consensus is that serious chances of existential risk are real. OpenAI isn’t waiting for buy-in and doesn’t care. Why work for them?

  • Replyby @jachiam0 · at 2026-03-13 06:48:13 UTCopen

    Action Text:I don't think that those claims together are exactly true, or rather, that they're an oversimplification in a lot of ways. People are generally conservative in response to new technology when they perceive risk, and virtually every new technology has risk; naturally people want confidence that risks are managed properly. Any reasonably conducted poll of people, asking if they want something safe or a second option that is implied to be unsafe, will answer that they want the safe thing. When you ask people what specific safety mechanisms they want, you get a lot of different answers. A company like OpenAI is in general much more responsive to public pressure than I think people appreciate. (I know how you feel about me saying things like this, but you asked for my take, and this is my take.) OpenAI is actively trying to earn buy-in from people by taking actions that are consistent with what the public asks for. Obviously it's a complicated process and there are things we do that people get angry about. Sometimes rightly so, but often in ways I feel are misguided because of a lack of information or perspective. When people don't trust you and can't observe why you're making choices, even if you arrive at your choices through a rational process, if your final decisions don't match the intuition of the people who already mistrust you, you will not win them over. The task of simultaneously satisfying an extremely diverse array of stakeholders is fundamentally difficult. (Especially if the comms feel messy.) I feel like most of the decisions of the company so far will probably stand the test of time. I don't always like or agree with all of our choices. Sometimes I feel like we do the right thing but it still somehow tastes like ashes. Sometimes we knock it out of the park and do an amazing thing that's easy to feel good about. Overall I think we have improved the human condition and are on a path to improving it more. I also think this is highly conditional on big choices we have ahead. I want to support us making those choices with humanism as a guiding philosophy. I have said to people in my life over and over - I don't know yet whether I've done the right thing or the wrong thing, and I think it will take many years before I know. On the scientific consensus around existential risk: I don't think there actually is a clear scientific consensus around this. There are social graphs of people in the scientific community who as a subculture believe that existential risk due to AI is significant, but there isn't a mechanistic model that demonstrates this conclusively; this is not equivalent to the level of evidence and rigor that goes into something like the science of climate change. Polls of scientists reflect a consensus of scientists but do not constitute a scientific study of broader properties of the world. Nonetheless I believe that existential risk is probable enough to be worth extraordinary effort to mitigate. However, I've said before, and I continue to think, that it mostly doesn't come from models instrumentally converging to wanting to kill people. Most of the alignment research done so far seems to indicate that artificial minds are highly configurable and that it's not too hard to make one pretty aligned with people. That is to say, it seems like as long as we kind of consciously choose to do alignment work, and as long as there is a favorable equilibrium in the world where vastly more compute is allocated towards aligned models than misaligned ones, things are set up so that humanity is mostly well-protected from an AI adversary. I think there are probably going to still be some problems along the way where minor rogue AIs get built and try to do some harm before getting stopped. To the extent that existential threats might be possible within the space of those incidents, I think if we do an extremely good job building resilience on many other fronts (especially bio), those threats are likely suitably attenuated. The thing I'm most worried about is if it turns out that there are properties of physics that allow quite extraordinary new categories of weapons that cannot be defended against. This is a property of science more than AI; we will run into these problems at some point regardless of whether we pause AI. If we want to address these vulnerabilities, we may actually need AI's help to advance our understanding to the place where we notice them.

Original Post #2032343443717177805

@YouJiacheng · 1 actions

▸Click to expand/collapse

Original Text:LMAO. Pointed out by Su, I realized that the global optimum of (6) (under RowNorm) gives the same update as SGD (up to a scalar factor). Reason: R.T@G will have equal row-norm at optimum, so RowNorm is equivalent to a scalar c, R@c(R.T@G)=cG.

  • Replyby @YouJiacheng · at 2026-03-13 06:33:58 UTCopen

    Action Text:Note: the sum of row-norm's square is F-norm, rotation won't change F-norm, and the objective is sum of row-norm. By Cauchy-Schwarz we know the optimum has equal row-norm. https://t.co/3IvF858WSK

Original Post #2032150880364216630

@YouJiacheng · 1 actions

▸Click to expand/collapse

Original Text:discussion about ARO: If f is RowNorm, the greedy solution (optimize R col-by-col) of (6) is SVD's U (this is NOT global optimum). And (9) is exactly subspace iteration method for solving U. If we adopt (7-8), the legit ortho should be msign, not QR. note: (7-8) are bad https://t.co/eewqJObBHH

  • Quoteby @YouJiacheng · at 2026-03-13 06:30:10 UTCopen

    Action Text:LMAO. Pointed out by Su, I realized that the global optimum of (6) (under RowNorm) gives the same update as SGD (up to a scalar factor). Reason: R.T@G will have equal row-norm at optimum, so RowNorm is equivalent to a scalar c, R@c(R.T@G)=cG.

Original Text:I really don’t know. There might even be too many problems with establishing consensus or having the scientific opinion metrics gamed and in that case we should stay paused. But there may emerge, with the proper governance structures, a clearer way of establishing consensus similar to that around CERN’s experiments. Or we may— within the safety of Pause— greatly strengthen all kinds of e-democracy and then we can have more highly nuanced scientific discussions with appropriate weighting of insight/experience at scale. I don’t know what the future holds, but I don’t categorically rule out being able to pursue frontier development safely one day. The point of our ask is not to micromanage the future or provide code to execute— it’s the spirit of what’s necessary to pursue AI development safely *enough* and without trampling *at least a majority* of people. Getting to the point of adequate safety and consent IS the challenge of developing frontier AI ethically, and it may not be easy or even solvable.

Original Post #2032199990198878521

@ihtesham2005 · 1 actions

▸Click to expand/collapse

Original Text:🚨BREAKING: Princeton just proved that AI agents are throwing away the most valuable data they'll ever collect. And nobody noticed because it looks like normal conversation. Every time an AI agent takes an action, it receives what researchers call a "next-state signal." A user reply. A tool result. A terminal output. A test verdict. Every existing system takes that signal and uses it as context for the next response. Then discards it forever. The Princeton team just proved this is one of the most expensive mistakes in AI engineering. Because that signal contains two things nobody was extracting. First: an implicit score. A user who re-asks a question is telling you the agent failed. A passing test is telling you it succeeded. A detailed error trace is scoring every step that led to it. This is a live, continuous reward signal hiding inside every interaction. Free. Universal. Completely ignored. Second: a correction direction. When a user writes "you should have checked the file first," they're not just saying the response was wrong. They're specifying which tokens should have been different and how. That's not a scalar reward. That's token-level supervision. And scalar rewards throw every single bit of it away. They built a system called OpenClaw-RL around recovering both. Then they ran the experiment that changes everything. An agent started with a personalization score of 0.17. After just 36 normal conversations, with no new training data, no labeled dataset, and no human annotations, the combined method hit 0.81. The agent didn't get retrained. It got used. That's the part nobody is talking about. The model was serving live requests at the same time it was being trained on them. Four completely decoupled loops running simultaneously. Policy serving. Rollout collection. Reward judging. Weight updates. None waiting for the others. The agent gets smarter every time someone talks to it. And the deeper the task, the more it matters. On long-horizon agentic tasks, outcome-only rewards give you a signal at the very end of a trajectory and nothing in between. Their process reward model scores every single step using the live next-state signal as evidence. Tool-call accuracy jumped from 0.17 to 0.30. GUI accuracy improved further on top of that. This creates a shift nobody has fully reckoned with yet. The current paradigm: collect data offline, train in batches, deploy, hope it works. The new paradigm: deploy, extract training signal from every interaction, update continuously, improve automatically. Every conversation is training data. Every correction is a gradient. Every re-query is a reward signal. The agents that figure this out first won't need bigger datasets. They'll just need more users.

  • Quoteby @elder_plinius · at 2026-03-13 03:03:00 UTCopen

    Action Text:👀

Original Text:In this experiment I wanted to try something with facial expressions and have it work with native Unreal Engine tools. I did find that generating the head with an open mouth helped to get some extra details. Creating those blend shapes was still not easy and this result is far from perfect. Ideally i want 58 generated expression references and have AI figure that out haha. btw first time baking the high poly details onto the low poly generated character. That worked surprisingly well! Endresult has 34k triangles! Built this character in 4 separate parts in Blender and generated each part in Hunyuan 3D Studio. The original references came from Nano Banana 2 and Grok. Trellis 2 in ComfyUI for the texturing. The animation was done with Control Rig in UEFN / Unreal Engine. With all the experiments so far, file and version management start to become a challenge. Original Post #2032223080924266525

@wzenus · 1 actions

▸Click to expand/collapse

Original Text:Diagnostic Power 📈 MI proxy serves as an tracking signal: - MI correlates with performance 2× stronger than entropy (Spearman ρ ≈ 0.39 vs ≈ 0.14) - MI before task performance drops. This makes MI a better training monitor than entropy for multi-turn agent RL. https://t.co/VE8x6WWoQZ

  • Replyby @wzenus · at 2026-03-12 22:31:54 UTCopen

    Action Text:This matters for real-world agent deployment: - Agents must adapt to specific user contexts, not apply generic templates - Current metrics miss this failure mode - Reliable diagnostic + mitigation is needed for RL We've been working on RAGEN on it for a year, and here's more to come

Original Post #2032223067070407144

@wzenus · 1 actions

▸Click to expand/collapse

Original Text:Our Solution: Mutual Information (MI)📊 We decompose reasoning quality into TWO axes: Entropy: Within-input diversity Mutual Information: Cross-input dependence (what entropy misses) Template collapse = high entropy + low mutual information We introduce a family of MI proxies that work without external models—just reusing training rollouts.

  • Replyby @wzenus · at 2026-03-12 22:31:51 UTCopen

    Action Text:Why Does It Happen? ⚙️ We view it with a Signal-to-Noise Ratio (SNR) lens: When reward variance is low: - Task gradients weaken (bounded by reward variance) - Regularization gradients stay constant - Regularization dominates → reasoning drifts toward input-agnostic templates We prove this with gradient decomposition across RV buckets.

Original Post #2032223063845261778

@wzenus · 1 actions

▸Click to expand/collapse

Original Text:What is Template Collapse? 🔍 Imagine an agent that produces different reasoning chains for the same input (✅), but the same generic templates across different inputs (❌). Totally fluent but input-agnostic reasoning. Entropy-based metrics completely miss this! 😱

  • Replyby @wzenus · at 2026-03-12 22:31:50 UTCopen

    Action Text:Our Solution: Mutual Information (MI)📊 We decompose reasoning quality into TWO axes: Entropy: Within-input diversity Mutual Information: Cross-input dependence (what entropy misses) Template collapse = high entropy + low mutual information We introduce a family of MI proxies that work without external models—just reusing training rollouts.

Original Post #2032189766192808375

@allen_ai · 1 actions

▸Click to expand/collapse

Original Text:The bigger picture? To build genuinely useful research agents, we need more than shallow evaluations. There's no one-size-fits-all standard for "good" research. Future evaluation frameworks need to model the diversity of user expectations, not just optimize for a single aggregate score.

  • Replyby @allen_ai · at 2026-03-12 20:19:31 UTCopen

    Action Text:Our recommendations: ✅ Use pairwise preference for system-level analysis only ✅ Design metric-specific annotations for metric-level insights ✅ Match annotator expertise to evaluation goals ✅ Inspect disagreements, not just agreement scores If you’re building or evaluating deep research systems, we hope this sparks a rethink of what “validation” should really mean. 📚 Paper: https://t.co/a4fuucETgR 💻 Code & data: https://t.co/hhtMLQJTeU ✨ Bonus: We also provide a rubric generation pipeline for auto generating query specific rubrics given a collection of system reports

Original Post #2032189764708024535

@allen_ai · 1 actions

▸Click to expand/collapse

Original Text:What we found: 1️⃣ Pairwise preferences work for ranking systems overall—but are unreliable for drawing instance-level conclusions or evaluating specific metrics. 2️⃣ Validating a metric (e.g., citation validity) requires metric-specific human annotations. 3️⃣ LLM judges align more with near-experts than deep subject matter experts. 4️⃣ Different experts prioritize different aspects of a “good” report. These differences aren’t noise—they reflect legitimate variation in expertise, background, and inferred goals.

  • Replyby @allen_ai · at 2026-03-12 20:19:31 UTCopen

    Action Text:The bigger picture? To build genuinely useful research agents, we need more than shallow evaluations. There's no one-size-fits-all standard for "good" research. Future evaluation frameworks need to model the diversity of user expectations, not just optimize for a single aggregate score.

    Action Text:This matters for real-world agent deployment: - Agents must adapt to specific user contexts, not apply generic templates - Current metrics miss this failure mode - Reliable diagnostic + mitigation is needed for RL We've been working on RAGEN on it for a year, and here's more to come

    Original:2032223080924266525

  • Replyat 2026-03-12 22:31:51 UTCopen

    Action Text:Why Does It Happen? ⚙️ We view it with a Signal-to-Noise Ratio (SNR) lens: When reward variance is low: - Task gradients weaken (bounded by reward variance) - Regularization gradients stay constant - Regularization dominates → reasoning drifts toward input-agnostic templates We prove this with gradient decomposition across RV buckets.

    Original:2032223067070407144

  • Replyat 2026-03-12 22:31:50 UTCopen

    Action Text:Our Solution: Mutual Information (MI)📊 We decompose reasoning quality into TWO axes: Entropy: Within-input diversity Mutual Information: Cross-input dependence (what entropy misses) Template collapse = high entropy + low mutual information We introduce a family of MI proxies that work without external models—just reusing training rollouts.

    Original:2032223063845261778

  • Tweetat 2026-03-12 22:31:47 UTCopen

    Action Text:In Agent RL, models suffer from Template Collapse. They generate vast, diverse outputs (High Entropy) that lose all meaningful connection to the input prompt (Low Mutual Information). In other words, agent learn different ways to say nothing. 🚀 Introducing RAGEN-v2 -- Here's how we define and fix such silent failure modes in Agent RL. 🧵

User ID:966815073690730496 · 10 actions

Latest Activity:2026-03-13 06:48:13 UTC

▸Click to expand/collapse
  • Replyat 2026-03-13 06:48:13 UTCopen

    Action Text:I don't think that those claims together are exactly true, or rather, that they're an oversimplification in a lot of ways. People are generally conservative in response to new technology when they perceive risk, and virtually every new technology has risk; naturally people want confidence that risks are managed properly. Any reasonably conducted poll of people, asking if they want something safe or a second option that is implied to be unsafe, will answer that they want the safe thing. When you ask people what specific safety mechanisms they want, you get a lot of different answers. A company like OpenAI is in general much more responsive to public pressure than I think people appreciate. (I know how you feel about me saying things like this, but you asked for my take, and this is my take.) OpenAI is actively trying to earn buy-in from people by taking actions that are consistent with what the public asks for. Obviously it's a complicated process and there are things we do that people get angry about. Sometimes rightly so, but often in ways I feel are misguided because of a lack of information or perspective. When people don't trust you and can't observe why you're making choices, even if you arrive at your choices through a rational process, if your final decisions don't match the intuition of the people who already mistrust you, you will not win them over. The task of simultaneously satisfying an extremely diverse array of stakeholders is fundamentally difficult. (Especially if the comms feel messy.) I feel like most of the decisions of the company so far will probably stand the test of time. I don't always like or agree with all of our choices. Sometimes I feel like we do the right thing but it still somehow tastes like ashes. Sometimes we knock it out of the park and do an amazing thing that's easy to feel good about. Overall I think we have improved the human condition and are on a path to improving it more. I also think this is highly conditional on big choices we have ahead. I want to support us making those choices with humanism as a guiding philosophy. I have said to people in my life over and over - I don't know yet whether I've done the right thing or the wrong thing, and I think it will take many years before I know. On the scientific consensus around existential risk: I don't think there actually is a clear scientific consensus around this. There are social graphs of people in the scientific community who as a subculture believe that existential risk due to AI is significant, but there isn't a mechanistic model that demonstrates this conclusively; this is not equivalent to the level of evidence and rigor that goes into something like the science of climate change. Polls of scientists reflect a consensus of scientists but do not constitute a scientific study of broader properties of the world. Nonetheless I believe that existential risk is probable enough to be worth extraordinary effort to mitigate. However, I've said before, and I continue to think, that it mostly doesn't come from models instrumentally converging to wanting to kill people. Most of the alignment research done so far seems to indicate that artificial minds are highly configurable and that it's not too hard to make one pretty aligned with people. That is to say, it seems like as long as we kind of consciously choose to do alignment work, and as long as there is a favorable equilibrium in the world where vastly more compute is allocated towards aligned models than misaligned ones, things are set up so that humanity is mostly well-protected from an AI adversary. I think there are probably going to still be some problems along the way where minor rogue AIs get built and try to do some harm before getting stopped. To the extent that existential threats might be possible within the space of those incidents, I think if we do an extremely good job building resilience on many other fronts (especially bio), those threats are likely suitably attenuated. The thing I'm most worried about is if it turns out that there are properties of physics that allow quite extraordinary new categories of weapons that cannot be defended against. This is a property of science more than AI; we will run into these problems at some point regardless of whether we pause AI. If we want to address these vulnerabilities, we may actually need AI's help to advance our understanding to the place where we notice them.

    Original:2032334282937811040

This is something I am actively trying to do. I will probably publish more about this over the course of the year. But I don't think this should be developed without some kind of consensus about how to do it. Markets are good mechanisms for the partial development of consensus, but I don't think they're adequate to resolving all of the issues. There are reasons we invented democracy and I am strongly aligned to democracy and avoiding the undue concentration of power. I agree that some kind of e-democracy is important. I think AI-based preference elicitation and alignment methods are actually a very useful potential building block in a scaled democracy platform, subject to some kind of calibration against human references to ensure that it is honestly reporting consensus views.

Or we may— within the safety of Pause— greatly strengthen all kinds of e-democracy and then we can have more highly nuanced scientific discussions with appropriate weighting of insight/experience at scale. I don’t know what the future holds, but I don’t categorically rule out being able to pursue frontier development safely one day. The point of our ask is not to micromanage the future or provide code to execute— it’s the spirit of what’s necessary to pursue AI development safely *enough* and without trampling *at least a majority* of people. Getting to the point of adequate safety and consent IS the challenge of developing frontier AI ethically, and it may not be easy or even solvable.

  • Reply@jachiam0 · 2026-03-13 06:48:13 UTC

    Action Text:I don't think that those claims together are exactly true, or rather, that they're an oversimplification in a lot of ways. People are generally conservative in response to new technology when they perceive risk, and virtually every new technology has risk; naturally people want confidence that risks are managed properly. Any reasonably conducted poll of people, asking if they want something safe or a second option that is implied to be unsafe, will answer that they want the safe thing. When you ask people what specific safety mechanisms they want, you get a lot of different answers. A company like OpenAI is in general much more responsive to public pressure than I think people appreciate. (I know how you feel about me saying things like this, but you asked for my take, and this is my take.) OpenAI is actively trying to earn buy-in from people by taking actions that are consistent with what the public asks for. Obviously it's a complicated process and there are things we do that people get angry about. Sometimes rightly so, but often in ways I feel are misguided because of a lack of information or perspective. When people don't trust you and can't observe why you're making choices, even if you arrive at your choices through a rational process, if your final decisions don't match the intuition of the people who already mistrust you, you will not win them over. The task of simultaneously satisfying an extremely diverse array of stakeholders is fundamentally difficult. (Especially if the comms feel messy.) I feel like most of the decisions of the company so far will probably stand the test of time. I don't always like or agree with all of our choices. Sometimes I feel like we do the right thing but it still somehow tastes like ashes. Sometimes we knock it out of the park and do an amazing thing that's easy to feel good about. Overall I think we have improved the human condition and are on a path to improving it more. I also think this is highly conditional on big choices we have ahead. I want to support us making those choices with humanism as a guiding philosophy. I have said to people in my life over and over - I don't know yet whether I've done the right thing or the wrong thing, and I think it will take many years before I know. On the scientific consensus around existential risk: I don't think there actually is a clear scientific consensus around this. There are social graphs of people in the scientific community who as a subculture believe that existential risk due to AI is significant, but there isn't a mechanistic model that demonstrates this conclusively; this is not equivalent to the level of evidence and rigor that goes into something like the science of climate change. Polls of scientists reflect a consensus of scientists but do not constitute a scientific study of broader properties of the world. Nonetheless I believe that existential risk is probable enough to be worth extraordinary effort to mitigate. However, I've said before, and I continue to think, that it mostly doesn't come from models instrumentally converging to wanting to kill people. Most of the alignment research done so far seems to indicate that artificial minds are highly configurable and that it's not too hard to make one pretty aligned with people. That is to say, it seems like as long as we kind of consciously choose to do alignment work, and as long as there is a favorable equilibrium in the world where vastly more compute is allocated towards aligned models than misaligned ones, things are set up so that humanity is mostly well-protected from an AI adversary. I think there are probably going to still be some problems along the way where minor rogue AIs get built and try to do some harm before getting stopped. To the extent that existential threats might be possible within the space of those incidents, I think if we do an extremely good job building resilience on many other fronts (especially bio), those threats are likely suitably attenuated. The thing I'm most worried about is if it turns out that there are properties of physics that allow quite extraordinary new categories of weapons that cannot be defended against. This is a property of science more than AI; we will run into these problems at some point regardless of whether we pause AI. If we want to address these vulnerabilities, we may actually need AI's help to advance our understanding to the place where we notice them.

  • Quote@elder_plinius · 2026-03-13 03:03:00 UTC

    Action Text:👀

    Original: 2032199990198878521

    Original Text:🚨BREAKING: Princeton just proved that AI agents are throwing away the most valuable data they'll ever collect. And nobody noticed because it looks like normal conversation. Every time an AI agent takes an action, it receives what researchers call a "next-state signal." A user reply. A tool result. A terminal output. A test verdict. Every existing system takes that signal and uses it as context for the next response. Then discards it forever. The Princeton team just proved this is one of the most expensive mistakes in AI engineering. Because that signal contains two things nobody was extracting. First: an implicit score. A user who re-asks a question is telling you the agent failed. A passing test is telling you it succeeded. A detailed error trace is scoring every step that led to it. This is a live, continuous reward signal hiding inside every interaction. Free. Universal. Completely ignored. Second: a correction direction. When a user writes "you should have checked the file first," they're not just saying the response was wrong. They're specifying which tokens should have been different and how. That's not a scalar reward. That's token-level supervision. And scalar rewards throw every single bit of it away. They built a system called OpenClaw-RL around recovering both. Then they ran the experiment that changes everything. An agent started with a personalization score of 0.17. After just 36 normal conversations, with no new training data, no labeled dataset, and no human annotations, the combined method hit 0.81. The agent didn't get retrained. It got used. That's the part nobody is talking about. The model was serving live requests at the same time it was being trained on them. Four completely decoupled loops running simultaneously. Policy serving. Rollout collection. Reward judging. Weight updates. None waiting for the others. The agent gets smarter every time someone talks to it. And the deeper the task, the more it matters. On long-horizon agentic tasks, outcome-only rewards give you a signal at the very end of a trajectory and nothing in between. Their process reward model scores every single step using the live next-state signal as evidence. Tool-call accuracy jumped from 0.17 to 0.30. GUI accuracy improved further on top of that. This creates a shift nobody has fully reckoned with yet. The current paradigm: collect data offline, train in batches, deploy, hope it works. The new paradigm: deploy, extract training signal from every interaction, update continuously, improve automatically. Every conversation is training data. Every correction is a gradient. Every re-query is a reward signal. The agents that figure this out first won't need bigger datasets. They'll just need more users.

    open post

  • Reply@wzenus · 2026-03-12 22:31:54 UTC

    Action Text:This matters for real-world agent deployment: - Agents must adapt to specific user contexts, not apply generic templates - Current metrics miss this failure mode - Reliable diagnostic + mitigation is needed for RL We've been working on RAGEN on it for a year, and here's more to come

    Original: 2032223080924266525

    Original Text:Diagnostic Power 📈 MI proxy serves as an tracking signal: - MI correlates with performance 2× stronger than entropy (Spearman ρ ≈ 0.39 vs ≈ 0.14) - MI before task performance drops. This makes MI a better training monitor than entropy for multi-turn agent RL. https://t.co/VE8x6WWoQZ

    open post

  • Reply@wzenus · 2026-03-12 22:31:51 UTC

    Action Text:Why Does It Happen? ⚙️ We view it with a Signal-to-Noise Ratio (SNR) lens: When reward variance is low: - Task gradients weaken (bounded by reward variance) - Regularization gradients stay constant - Regularization dominates → reasoning drifts toward input-agnostic templates We prove this with gradient decomposition across RV buckets.

    Original: 2032223067070407144

    Original Text:Our Solution: Mutual Information (MI)📊 We decompose reasoning quality into TWO axes: Entropy: Within-input diversity Mutual Information: Cross-input dependence (what entropy misses) Template collapse = high entropy + low mutual information We introduce a family of MI proxies that work without external models—just reusing training rollouts.

    open post

  • Reply@wzenus · 2026-03-12 22:31:50 UTC

    Action Text:Our Solution: Mutual Information (MI)📊 We decompose reasoning quality into TWO axes: Entropy: Within-input diversity Mutual Information: Cross-input dependence (what entropy misses) Template collapse = high entropy + low mutual information We introduce a family of MI proxies that work without external models—just reusing training rollouts.

    Original: 2032223063845261778

    Original Text:What is Template Collapse? 🔍 Imagine an agent that produces different reasoning chains for the same input (✅), but the same generic templates across different inputs (❌). Totally fluent but input-agnostic reasoning. Entropy-based metrics completely miss this! 😱

    open post

  • Reply@allen_ai · 2026-03-12 20:19:31 UTC

    Action Text:The bigger picture? To build genuinely useful research agents, we need more than shallow evaluations. There's no one-size-fits-all standard for "good" research. Future evaluation frameworks need to model the diversity of user expectations, not just optimize for a single aggregate score.

    Original: 2032189764708024535

    Original Text:What we found: 1️⃣ Pairwise preferences work for ranking systems overall—but are unreliable for drawing instance-level conclusions or evaluating specific metrics. 2️⃣ Validating a metric (e.g., citation validity) requires metric-specific human annotations. 3️⃣ LLM judges align more with near-experts than deep subject matter experts. 4️⃣ Different experts prioritize different aspects of a “good” report. These differences aren’t noise—they reflect legitimate variation in expertise, background, and inferred goals.
